      <h1 class="p-name">
                  What Beautiful HTML Code Looks&nbsp;Like              </h1>

            <div class="article-content-meta">

     <div class="author-avatar">
    <a href="https://css-tricks.com/author/chriscoyier/">
      <img alt='Photo of Chris Coyier' src='https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/8081b26e05bb4354f7d65ffc34cbbd67?s=80&#038;d=retro&#038;r=pg' srcset='https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/8081b26e05bb4354f7d65ffc34cbbd67?s=160&#038;d=retro&#038;r=pg 2x' class='avatar avatar-80 photo' height='80' width='80' />    </a>

      <div class="sharedaddy sd-sharing-enabled"><div class="robots-nocontent sd-block sd-social sd-social-text sd-sharing"><h3 class="sd-title">Share this:</h3><div class="sd-content"><ul><li class="share-twitter"><a rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer" data-shared="sharing-twitter-121" class="share-twitter sd-button no-icon" href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/?share=twitter" target="_blank" title="Click to share on Twitter"><span>Twitter</span></a></li><li class="share-facebook"><a rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer" data-shared="sharing-facebook-121" class="share-facebook sd-button no-icon" href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/?share=facebook" target="_blank" title="Click to share on Facebook"><span>Facebook</span></a></li><li class="share-end"></li></ul></div></div></div>

      <div class="article-and-sidebar on-light">
      
        <div class="article-content">
          
                    
                    
          <div class="explanation">
<p>I originally wrote this over two years ago. It was getting a little long in the tooth, especially now that HTML5 has come along and made HTML far more beautiful than even XHTML 1.1 was. So I updated it!</p>
</div>
<p>I can't help but <em>view source</em> on every nice looking website I see. It's like if you had x-ray glasses that allowed you to see any person you ever saw in their underwear at will. How could you not? It's just so tempting to see if a beautiful website is built with beautiful code as well, or if its beauty if only skin-deep. Code? Beautiful? Sure. After all, <em>Code is Poetry</em>. This is just HTML, so it can't be quite as intricate and elegant as a dynamic language, but it still bears the brush strokes of its creator. </p>
<p>It gets me to thinking, what makes beautiful code? <strong>In HTML, it comes down to craftsmanship.</strong> Let's take a look at some markup written they way markup should be written and see how beautiful it can be.</p>
<p><a href="http://css-tricks.com/examples/CleanCode/Beautiful-HTML.png"><img src="//css-tricks.com/wp-content/csstricks-uploads/Beautiful-HTML-small.jpg" width="570" height="570" alt="" title="" /></a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><a href="http://css-tricks.com/examples/CleanCode/Beautiful-HTML.png" class="button">Large PNG</a> &nbsp; <a href="http://css-tricks.com/examples/CleanCode/Beautiful-HTML.psd" class="button">Original PSD</a> &nbsp; <a href="http://css-tricks.com/examples/CleanCode/Beautiful-HTML.txt" class="button">Text of HTML</a></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>It's big enough to print out and tape up inside your locker to impress your friends. Well, it might be a bit of an awkward size. I'll make the PSD available in case you want to alter it.</p>
<p><span id="more-121"></span></p>
<ul id="beaut-list">
<li><strong>HTML5</strong> - HTML5 and it's new elements make for the most beautiful HTML yet.</li>
<li><strong>DOCTYPE</strong> - HTML5 has the best DOCTYPE ever</li>
<li><strong>Indentation</strong> - Code is indented to show parent/child relationships and emphasize hierarchy.</li>
<li><strong>Charset</strong> - Declared as first thing in the head, before any content.</li>
<li><strong>Title</strong> - Title of the site is simple and clean. Purpose of page is first, a separator is used, and ends with title of the site.</li>
<li><strong>CSS</strong> - Only one single stylesheet is used (media types declared inside stylesheet), and only served to good browsers. IE 6 and below are served a universal stylesheet.</li>
<li><strong>Body</strong> - ID applied to body to allow for unique page styling without any additional markup.</li>
<li><strong>JavaScript</strong> - jQuery (the most beautiful JavaScript library) is served from Google. Only a single JavaScript file is loaded. Both scripts are referenced at the bottom of the page.</li>
<li><strong>File Paths</strong> - Site resources use relative file paths for efficiency. Content file paths are absolute, assuming content is syndicated.</li>
<li><strong>Image Attributes</strong> - Images include alternate text, mostly for visually impaired uses but also for validation. Height and width applied for rendering efficiency.</li>
<li><strong>Main Content First</strong> - The main content of the page comes after basic identity and navigation but before any ancillary content like sidebar material.</li>
<li><strong>Appropriate Descriptive Block-Level Elements</strong> - Header, Nav, Section, Article, Aside... all appropriately describe the content they contain better than the divs of old.</li>
<li><strong>Hierarchy</strong> - Title tags are reserved for real content, and follow a clear hierarchy.</li>
<li><strong>Appropriate Descriptive Tags</strong> - Lists are marked up as lists, depending on the needs of the list: unordered, ordered, and the underused definition list.</li>
<li><strong>Common Content Included</strong> - Things common across multiple pages are inserted via server side includes (via whatever method, language, or CMS that works for you)</li>
<li><strong>Semantic Classes</strong> - Beyond appropriate element names, classes and IDs are semantic: they describe without specifying. (e.g. "col" is much better than "left")</li>
<li><strong>Classes</strong> - Are used any time similar styling needs to be applied to multiple elements.</li>
<li><strong>IDs</strong> - Are used any time an element appears only once on the page and cannot be targeted reasonably any other way. </li>
<li><strong>Dynamic Elements</strong> - Things that need to be dynamic, are dynamic.</li>
<li><strong>Characters Encoded</strong> - If it's a special character, it's encoded.</li>
<li><strong>Free From Styling</strong> - Nothing on the page applies styling or even implies what the styling might be. Everything on the page is either a required site resource, content, or describing content.</li>
<li><strong>Comments</strong> - Comments are included for things that may not be immediately obvious upon reviewing the code.</li>
<li><strong>Valid</strong> - The code should adhere to W3C guidelines. Tags are closed, required attributes used, nothing deprecated, etc.</li>
</ul>
          
          <div class="sharedaddy sd-sharing-enabled"><div class="robots-nocontent sd-block sd-social sd-social-text sd-sharing"><h3 class="sd-title">Share this:</h3><div class="sd-content"><ul><li class="share-twitter"><a rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer" data-shared="sharing-twitter-121" class="share-twitter sd-button no-icon" href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/?share=twitter" target="_blank" title="Click to share on Twitter"><span>Twitter</span></a></li><li class="share-facebook"><a rel="nofollow noopener noreferrer" data-shared="sharing-facebook-121" class="share-facebook sd-button no-icon" href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/?share=facebook" target="_blank" title="Click to share on Facebook"><span>Facebook</span></a></li><li class="share-end"></li></ul></div></div></div>        
        </div>

            <p>All good (and it should be obvious), but the idea of &#8220;Code is Tabbed into Sections&#8221; is just nonsense.</p>
<p>The page code is for the machines not humans. It shouldn&#8217;t matter if it&#8217;s tabbed, messy or even all in one line. Actually for IE6 is sometimes better to keep lists in one line, without line breaks.</p>
<p>Perhaps tabbing is good if you write all code per page by hand, but if you use CMS then most likely you&#8217;ll not achieve all pretty formatting. </p>
<p>And there are always tools to hand to quickly format page source code to see &#8220;how they&#8217;ve done it&#8221; even if the original HTML is &#8220;messy&#8221;.</p>

    <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-704">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-704">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/e9a63ef61611a91c600fefda639b1b0e?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Niels</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-704"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2007-11-07T05:11:34-07:00">November 7, 2007</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Hi everyone,</p>
<p>Good article, good comments.<br />
I&#8217;ve also been wondering about cleaner code but I bumped into some obstacles I still haven&#8217;t found a good solution for.</p>
<p>1: how should we combine PHP HTML and Javascript in one file? If I code PHP, I try to use the MVC model and use only some loops and echo statements in the &#8220;view&#8221; pages. But if I then also add JS to the mix, it quickly gets chaotic. </p>
<p>2:If you want your PHP to produce clean well indented HTML you have to code in /t and /n by hand in your PHP. That makes your PHP less clean and readable. Because the PHP code is more likely to produce complex problems for which I want the most clean code I prefer to have less clean HTML.</p>
<p>3: A problem I bumped into recently. If you have a site where you let your users add content and you let them use certain tags, how do you control that the tags used are also clean?</p>
<p>What are your thoughts on these questions?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64519">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64519">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/55af02ec22fd4005261ed2088534f194?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Lee Kowalkowski</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64519"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T14:26:11-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>1: Why one file?  Different application tiers have different MVC uses, you can have views on a database tier, but those views form the model for the tier above.  Same goes for the client tier, the server serves its view (the HTML) and that is the browser page&#8217;s model.  Presentation should be governed by CSS if applying MVC.</p>
<p>2: You can use the heredoc syntax in PHP, and your PHP will be a lot cleaner and very readable.</p>
<p>3: That&#8217;s application logic, you can always try to format and validate it, should be fun.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-723">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-723">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/27a1a19819559cc53802365cbd17734a?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">John Vance</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-723"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2007-11-07T16:55:30-07:00">November 7, 2007</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>No PHP includes, jsp:includes, or any of that other rot.  If you decide you don&#8217;t want to include the content at that particular location, or at all, then guess what &#8211; you get to change all 150 pages of your site.</p>
<p>No, instead, use a decorator/compositor, like <a href="http://www.opensymphony.com/sitemesh/" rel="nofollow">sitemesh</a>, or a mvc framework like RoR that provides view templating for free.</p>
<p>Don&#8217;t include headers and footers into your page &#8211; include your page into your headers and footers!</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>
      
    
  </li><!-- #comment-## -->

          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/4929306ba4694bd6a2783f7353199aae?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

            <div class="comment-author">brad</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-945"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2007-11-13T12:43:34-07:00">November 13, 2007</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>To clarify about IE and xhtml. IE defaults to quirks mode with an xhtml 1.1 doctype but does not with xhtml1.0 strict.</p>
<p>Secondly, it&#8217;s semantic if it can be read. Having an extra div around a ul isn&#8217;t going to kill anyone. Although, I personally wouldn&#8217;t do the extra divs though.</p>
<p>Lastly, tabbing the code helps other coders &#8211; html isn&#8217;t for machines only &#8211;  someone may need to go in and work with it at a later point whether in a cms our outside, it helps immensely.</p>
<p>I feel for you Chris, I have the same addiction to well crafted code.</p>

    <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-1044">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-1044">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/74e406d9db1d3663cddec50f0c46ffe6?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Thibaut Allender</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-1044"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2007-11-15T03:19:36-07:00">November 15, 2007</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Hi,</p>
<p>Good advices, but i&#8217;d rather (and I do it) put a class on the body, instead of an ID.</p>
<p>The body tag is already unique, so body.yourclass will be unique, no need for an ID.</p>
<p>Plus, the ID you choose for your body could conflict with an ID used in the entire page.</p>
<p>To conclude, we could also say that in *one* website, the different pages bodies are the same &#8220;object&#8221;. Giving them an ID would mean &#8220;each body is a different entity&#8221;, which is not true : same entity but with little (or big) differences, so the class is semanticaly better, to my opinion.</p>
<p>My 2 cents&#8230;</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/dd6a7f98fc0d32f5a61fc12721a21312?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

            <div class="comment-author">Lee</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64440"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-09T18:54:08-07:00">November 9, 2009</time></div>

            <p>In this case, using an id on the body element makes sense.  It&#8217;s used to indicate the specific page being loaded.  this can be useful if you need to override the default CSS for a single page, now you have an id you can use in your selector statement.</p>

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64483">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64483">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/9a9780032e2a85f5cd04f6a999982dc5?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">David</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64483"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:01:11-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I disagree.</p>
<p>A body only appears once, so there&#8217;s no reason you <strong>can&#8217;t</strong> use an ID instead of a class.</p>
<p>As far as why you <strong>should</strong> use an ID instead of a class is stated in the article/image. It&#8217;s for applying particular unique styling to a particular unique page.</p>
<p>Now, if one were to have a subset of multiple pages styled this way, a class would be appropriate.</p>
<p>Say, for example, you want a certain different look for your online catalog versus your blog. All the pages for the catalog could have a body class of &#8220;catalog&#8221; and get a green background.</p>
<p>But then, you have a page in the catalog of sale items, give that body an id of &#8220;red_hot&#8221; and style the background red, since it&#8217;s unique.</p>
<p>Some of this is unnecessary when using a CMS, but if you want to avoid loading extra stylesheets, or you want to give special treatment via JavaScript, an id and/or class on the body can provide a very useful <strong>hook</strong> into your markup.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-1333">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-1333">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/a447c5b472c20db266fb1fc0365870b0?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">J. Bradford Dillon</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-1333"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2007-11-20T11:39:50-07:00">November 20, 2007</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>@John Lascurettes:</p>
<p>While you&#8217;re technically right, in regards to not needing a wrapping div, it&#8217;s still good to do for several reasons.</p>
<p>Just one example: planning for the future. The beauty of CSS is that you can markup a document semantically, and the presentation layer is kept separate. in an ideal world, that means you can markup a page once, and design and re-design to your heart&#8217;s content. You might not need a wrapping div NOW, but it doesn&#8217;t hurt, it adds more control, and you might change your mind.</p>

          </div>
        </div>

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64484">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64484">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/9a9780032e2a85f5cd04f6a999982dc5?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">David</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64484"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:07:49-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>1) You&#8217;ve presented zero reasons why this kind of markup would <strong>not</strong> work. It would simply have more elements, and probably more dynamic content.</p>
<p>2) Commenting is mentioned and highlighted in both the image and the article.</p>
<p>To be honest, html comments are much less important that code comments. And by code, I mean <em>real</em> code (PHP, JavaScript, etc), not markup (HTML).</p>
<p>The only reason to use an HTML comment (as illustrated in the article) is if it&#8217;s not completely self evident what the code is doing. Like the php includes.</p>
<p>Example: You can see the site, and see the markup&#8230; any real reason you need a comment that says &#8220;this is the main navigation menu&#8221; for a UL element with the class of &#8220;main_nav_menu&#8221;?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>
      
    
  </li><!-- #comment-## -->

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-4649">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/8d65b31ceb1645dd8001c8b153014339?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">imsuden</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-4649"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2008-01-16T10:14:01-07:00">January 16, 2008</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I don&#8217;t agree with all of these.  A lot of these features are used to force your HTML/CSS to over-reach itself (e.g. wrapping up a page in a DIV serves no semantic purpose at all &#8211; it just helps designers achieve graphical/typographical effects).  Similarly, the point of putting a menu in a list is that it is just that &#8211; a list; not so you can add rounded corners to your tabs.</p>
<p>Also, isn&#8217;t it a better idea to keep your headers logically organised and styled consistently?  Why change your level 2 headings from page to page &#8211; it just confuses the user.</p>
<p>Beautiful code should be well structured and marked up semantically.  That is what makes it easy to read.</p>

          </div>
        </div>

            <div class="comment-author">Lee Kowalkowski</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64521"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T14:38:18-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>

          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Heading levels are announced by screen readers, they can navigate by them too.  So if you&#8217;re listening to a page, it starts by reading all the guff in the header, you can just press the key for H1 or H2 to skip the jibber-jabber and start hearing the content actually relevant to the page.  Consistent use of heading levels across your site will be a godsend to a blind user.</p>

    <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-7050">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-7050">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/f5a32c590290c9a7f9b72e0dda3ade21?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Robert</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-7050"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2008-02-23T19:17:37-07:00">February 23, 2008</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I actually would disagree with nearly everything in this article. It misses the point entirely. The point of HTML css and other related technologies is to produce the website and then usually keep the website up to date. The point of HTML css and so on is not the end in itself. People don&#8217;t pay you to write code they pay you to solve a problem. Thus they want the problem solved without generating new problems. So creating the cleanest simplest code possible that does exactly the work required is what most people want. So in reading the above article I would ask the scientific questions of why and prove it for each of the points. </p>
<p># DOCTYPE Properly Declared<br />
Why? Most web pages will be totally fine without this thus it adds unneeded clutter. Plus the w3 people are really ticked withevery pointing to their site. They say if you have to use this then put the .dtd file on your own server and use your own damn bandwidth.<br />
# Tidy Head Section<br />
I agree. Keep things tidy like this says.</p>
<p># Body IDed<br />
Why? What does this accomplish as compared to not using it. Personally I can think of specific reasons to id the body such as if you are going to dynamically alter it. But otherwise you end up with code that might do nothing.<br />
# Semantically Clean Menu<br />
Why? If the menu is likely to change then this might be a good rule. But depending on the menu structure and page design this might not be a good idea.<br />
# Main DIV for all Page Content<br />
Why. This might be code for the sake of coding. What problem does this solve?<br />
# Important Content First<br />
Why? SEO maybe? Western people read left to right and top to bottom. Thus in a somewhat standard 3 column layout I would expect Header, left, center, right, footer to be the code order anything else and I am hunting around.</p>
<p># Common Content INCLUDED<br />
This I somewhat agree with. Generally a good idea but sometimes this will work and others it won&#8217;t. It is an architecture issue.<br />
# Code is Tabbed into Sections<br />
Oh I love properly tabbed code. Tabs are my friend. On an offt opic note Python uses tabs as its method of structure. Thus code tabbed in will be the same as braced code in most other languages that use braces.</p>
<p># Proper Ending Tags<br />
Yes but I really hate people who put that little /&gt; at the image tag among others. It works fine without it and no major browser has the slightest problem without it.</p>
<p># Hierarchy of Header Tags<br />
SEO has its own set of rules that are ruining header tags for everyone. But once again clients are not paying for nice code they are running a business and SEO is generally very important to their bottom line thus it superceeds all good design rules.<br />
# Content, Content, Content<br />
P vs BR is more of a style choice. If you plan on noodling P in your CSS then it can be better but if you don&#8217;t then why not stick with a tag more inline with how computers think?<br />
# No Styling!<br />
Wrong wrong wrong. CSS is a tool like all the others. Tables font and other tags are just tools. A table can go a million miles with few surprises that CSS will kill you with. But again it is an architecture issue. If you have hundreds of hand coded html pages then CSS will save you during your next site redesign but if you only have a few templates in a database driven site then putting too much of your formatting in css will just get in your way and increase code complexity.</p>
<p>My rule is to use the right tool for the job not my favorite tool for the job. The right tool should result in the fewest number of small simple files. A good example of where I have seen bad use of things like CSS is where you have say 30 fundamentally different pages in a site and all their css is clumped into one file. Just as bad would be to have the css broken up in 30 separate files.<br />
CSS in a separate file serves three purposes. A common repository for common code, potentially a single place to reformat the entire site and to speed the loading of the site as the the css file will be 304&#8217;d. So if the css is not repeated throughout the site then that only leaves speeding up page load times. So if you are facing a common situation of copying some CSS into a CSS file that is used in only one place on a page infrequently loaded why is it in the css file? Why not go so far as to put the CSS into a style attribute right where it gets used? Css purists will get all wound up over this one but to me this reeks of religion not fact. In good programming you want to keep in mind that real people will read this code. If a person is in making changes to a page and they have to jump back and fourth to a css file and hunt for some tags modifiers then have you served the purposes of your client or have you served the purposes of some rules that someone made up in their head? If the style modifiers are right where you need to make your changes then you have made everything easier, simpler, and thus cheaper.</p>
<p>Personally I would fire any programmers that I found wasting my money on &#8220;proper&#8221; formatting that only served to complicate and obfuscate the code and thus miss the whole point as to why they were hired in the first place; Solving problems.<br />
Much more important code rules that I impose are like: Use English in code. ftr_l.png is bad where footer_left.png is better.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">David</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64490"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:32:02-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>First, I completely disagree with nearly every single thing you just said.</p>
<p># DOCTYPE Properly Declared<br />
A) Doctypes exist for a reason. If you don&#8217;t understand, perhaps you should google.</p>
<p># Body IDed<br />
A) Explained in an above comment of mine. It&#8217;s for control over, and hooks into, your markup.</p>
<p># Semantically Clean Menu<br />
A) Everything should be semantically clean. This isn&#8217;t just about your &#8220;made for IE 6&#8221; site looking alright or your Frontpage/Dreamweaver code working&#8230; it&#8217;s about longevity, stability, and compatibility of markup.</p>
<p># Main DIV for all Page Content<br />
A) This is, currently, the only way to solve certain design problems like fixed-width, centered websites.</p>
<p># Important Content First<br />
A) Again, previous comment. Accessibility. Screenreader users shouldn&#8217;t have to listen to your whole blogroll or list of probably unrelated corporate services just to get to an article.</p>
<p>And, why NOT put the important content first? I&#8217;ve yet to hear a valid reason why you would purposefully not do this, other than developer laziness.</p>
<p># Proper Ending Tags<br />
A) An image tag is, by definition, a self closing tag. Like link tag and meta tags. Not sure why you&#8217;re arguing with the rules. It&#8217;s like saying, yeah, but I hate using if/else code in programming&#8230; i&#8217;d rather use turtle/shell o_0</p>
<p># Hierarchy of Header Tags<br />
A) People who blindly read and follow SEO crap are their own worst enemies. Valid, semantic code, and useful, relevant content are the only two things you ever need to worry about. The rest is all diminishing returns, especially if you&#8217;re paying some &#8220;SEO expert&#8221; to muck up your site.</p>
<p># P vs BR is more of a style choice.<br />
A) No, actually it&#8217;s not. It&#8217;s a semantic and structural choice. If something is a paragraph, you use the P tag, if you just want to break a line of text, use the br tag. Pretty simple, really.</p>
<p># putting too much of your formatting in css will just get in your way and increase code complexity<br />
A) No, just plain wrong. Using table for something that&#8217;s not tabular data is just ignorant. Using CSS doesn&#8217;t complicate things, at all &#8212; it greatly simplifies things.</p>
<p>Ever hear of DRY? Reusable code? Modularity? Ease of maintenance?</p>
<p># put the CSS into a style attribute right where it gets used&#8230; reeks of religion not fact<br />
A) That&#8217;s potentially the dumbest thing I&#8217;ve ever heard a supposedly smart person say.</p>
<p>Second, I lost all hope in have meaningful debate with you on the topic once I saw this:</p>
<p>&#8220;wasting my money on proper formatting that only served to complicate and obfuscate the code &#8221;</p>
<p>Proper formatting obfuscates the code? Really?</p>
<p>I wouldn&#8217;t mind being fired by you for that reason, but it also makes me really, <strong>REALLY</strong> thankful I don&#8217;t work for someone like you.</p>

    <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-7062">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-7062">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/b0220a4b0ec42f2ddc610d0c187b1ee1?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Thomas Thomassen</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-7062"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2008-02-24T01:35:51-07:00">February 24, 2008</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>@Robert:</p>
<p>DOCTYPE: Leaving it out triggers quirks mode with many odd behaviours making it hard to produse cross browser implementations as quick mode in other browsers tried to implement IE&#8217;s many quicks. And this makes it all a guessing game.<br />
And where have you heard that W3C don&#8217;t want people to use a doctype with DTD relating to the w3c site? There&#8217;s no browser that loads the DTD of HTML files, so it got no grounds for that statement.</p>
<p>Semantically Clean Menu:<br />
It&#8217;s not just a semantically clean menu that&#8217;s important, but the whole document. And the reason being is to make machines understand and interpret the content. Assistive technologies, search engines are the biggest groups which benefit from semantic documents.</p>
<p>Important Content First:<br />
Because a website might be represented in so many ways. A mobile phone, for instance, will not be able to display a three column layout. It will need everything linearised. Websites is being accessed by so much more than just a browser on a computer. That&#8217;s why the layout shouldn&#8217;t dictate the order of your content.</p>
<p>Proper Ending Tags<br />
The /&gt; is there because it&#8217;s in XHTML format. HTML in XML format. If you don&#8217;t like that use HTML.<br />
The benefit XHTML has over HTML is that you can use a simple XML parser to read and iterate over the DOM. If you have HTML you need an SMGL parser which is not so simple.</p>
<p>Hierarchy of Header Tags<br />
&#8220;SEO has its own set of rules that are ruining header tags for everyone.&#8221; &#8211;And what would that be?</p>
<p># Content, Content, Content<br />
P vs BR is NOT a style choice. A P element defines a paragraph, a BR is simply a forced linebreak. How is BR more inline with how computers think?</p>
<p># No Styling!<br />
Tables for layout and font tags are the wrong tools, period. It&#8217;s all about separating the layout from the content.</p>
<p>By the whole it appears that you see websites as a purely visual matter. Your arguments doesn&#8217;t accommodate how to present the document to different media, such as screen readers, handheld devices and print. Leaving all the layout into separate CSS files allows you to serve a different CSS to these different medias.<br />
If a site has 30 pages that need 30 different stylesheets then the real question is why does it require 30 different stylesheet? Why is there no consistency?</p>
<p>&#8220;Personally I would fire any programmers that I found wasting my money on &#8220;proper&#8221; formatting that only served to complicate and obfuscate the code and thus miss the whole point as to why they were hired in the first place; Solving problems.&#8221;<br />
CSS doesn&#8217;t complicate or obfuscate. That would be solely due to the person writing the codes.</p>
<p>Some of your questions are valid if you look at the articles as a spesific example. Several of the DIVs and IDs might not be needed. It&#8217;d all depend on each site and the markup should be adapted to each case.<br />
However, many of you questions regarding semantics and separating layout from content doesn&#8217;t hold water. Have you read up on the benefits of web semantics? The value of separating content, layout and behaviour?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">

        <div class="author-avatar">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Stevie D</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-7283"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2008-02-26T16:48:09-07:00">February 26, 2008</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>&#8220;In general, I find that expressing your page with the fewest (and no fewer) elements possible to be most preferred.&#8221;</p>
<p>Yes &#8211; with a caveat. Sometimes it&#8217;s useful to add an extra div in places where it <em>might</em> be useful as a styling hook at a later date. A couple of times I&#8217;ve cursed myself for not putting any spare styling hooks in when I&#8217;ve wanted to tweak something, and have had to either not bother, or go through every page adding them. A few spare divs round key chunks of the page won&#8217;t hurt, and could save you a lot of time in the long run.</p>
<p>&#8220;No, no, no. Why would you even bother to set the character set, then, if youre not going to use the characters in it? Just put the  symbol directly in your code, a practice that has it show up properly as long as youve set the proper charset.&#8221;</p>
<p>No, no, no. Do you know what charset your text editor is using? I don&#8217;t &#8211; it could be Windows-1252 for all I know. Typing UTF-8 into the third line of a plain text document doesn&#8217;t magically save that document in UTF-8! I&#8217;d rather stay safe and use the character encoding, and guarantee that it will come out right in every browser.</p>

    <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-8708">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-8708">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/52ec3d32605d3a54f7632023a249ff02?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64493">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64493">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/9a9780032e2a85f5cd04f6a999982dc5?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>

        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">David</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64493"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:34:50-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>You&#8217;d want to include the content before, say, your sidebar, using a typical blog as an example.</p>
<p>The main reason is Accessibility.</p>
<p>A person with a screenreader (which reads linearly down the page) should not have to skip through your 50-item list of categories and blogroll just to get to your article.</p>

    <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-12454">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-12454">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/6aca2dfb626e4a1b855c38cc381b66db?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Yvan256</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-12454"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2008-04-06T09:35:38-07:00">April 6, 2008</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Very nice article, however I might suggest another tip to your list: use the right graphic file format for the right image type.</p>
<p>Your &#8220;CleanCode&#8221; image should obviously be PNG (or GIF), NOT JPEG. JPEG is for photographic content, and your CleanCode image is clearly not a photo!</p>
<p>I did some tests, and you should be able to make an 8-bit PNG with 32 colors at around 100-125 KiB, with a better end result than your 896 KiB JPEG file. The same could probably be done with the PDF if you embed a PNG instead of a JPEG.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64394">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/697c7f4ee06b6c547dd951aef547e439?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Russell Heimlich</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64394"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-09T08:04:43-07:00">November 9, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>You could simplify your IE CSS a lot more by using conditional comments to render divs with ID of the IE version and a class of IE. This way you can keep all of your styles together in one stylesheet. Makes it a lot easier to manage. Look at the source code of newsweek.com to see what I mean. </p>
<p>I&#8217;m assuming you&#8217;re using a reset stylesheet in your main.css file. This is fine for a small site, but bigger sites need stylesheets broken out more for flexibility.</p>

          </div>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Simon</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64596"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-11T19:31:47-07:00">November 11, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>As stated to prevent blocking (slow down), that said you can use parallel loading of your JS files, whilst still in the head of your document to achieve the same thing.</p>
<p>I personally use parallel loading, in the bottom of my document, not only ensuring faster loading of content, but faster rendering and zero blocking.</p>
<p>In fact my scripts appends my scripts into the head of the document, which means it still looks correct in things like firebug.</p>
<p>x</p>

    <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-64403">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64403">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/c6f03f504ee24f3098413dd32626118a?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Martin</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64403"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-09T09:33:16-07:00">November 9, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>The only thing I would be inclined to do differently, is the location of your H1 tag. I normally put it around the site logo or title, if it has one. In this case it would come in the line that says:</p>
<pre>&lt;a href="/" rel="nofollow">Site Title&lt;/a></pre>
<p>That way, if your page is viewed or printed without styles, you will still have a nice big bold H1 site title at the top of your page. The remaining headings can then start with H2, H3, etc. It may not be strictly semantically correct, but isn&#8217;t H1 the closest thing we have (apart from TITLE, which does not belong in the body) for semantically describing the site title?</p>
<p>I thought it might also be useful for SEO purposes, but I could be wrong, if the title tag has the same content and takes higher precedence for SEO. Anyone know the answer to this?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I was about to ask the same. Surely if you are writing your pages in UTF-8 then it makes sense to write the raw character rather than the character code? It makes sense from two points of view: you arent inflating the file size with stuff you dont need; and you can read what youve written without having to parse character codes in your head!</p>
<p>If you&#8217;re going to write all the character codes out, then theres really no point in using UTF-8.</p>

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Ian</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64442"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-09T19:36:26-07:00">November 9, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Those script tags aren&#8217;t &#8220;stewn about wildly,&#8221; they&#8217;re specifically put at the bottom of the page so they don&#8217;t block other parts of the page from downloading, thus decreasing the amount of time it takes for the page to render.</p>
<p><a href="http://developer.yahoo.com/performance/rules.html#js_bottom" rel="nofollow">Check out what Yahoo has to say on the matter.</a></p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>
      
    
  </li><!-- #comment-## -->

    <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-67518">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-67518">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/aef8f95716a44d56f18b21bceee9b593?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">cgrymala</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-67518"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2010-01-05T12:00:44-07:00">January 5, 2010</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Title tags are certainly not a requirement, but they can be extremely useful for accessibility purposes, especially when dealing with links.</p>
<p>Screen readers are capable of skipping from link-to-link. Therefore, if the text of your link does not make sense all by itself, then you need a title tag to help add context to it.</p>
<p>That said, I&#8217;ve been told it&#8217;s worse to add titles if they are identical to the link text. Therefore, something like:</p>
<p><code>You can &lt;a href="http://www.example.com/" title="View an article about clean HTML"&gt;read the article&lt;/a&gt; if you feel like it.</code></p>
<p>is good, but something like:</p>
<p><code>You can &lt;a href="http://www.example.com/" title="View an article about clean HTML"&gt;View an article about clean HTML&lt;/a&gt;</code></p>
<p>is bad. Title tags should only be used to add context where it is missing.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64461">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/d7c58d7270127a933ea4ca10026a46e5?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Mark</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64461"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T02:19:02-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Cool, I love the code formatting, it looks really great! </p>
<p>But.. this is very useless and a waste of our bandwidth. HTML == output, not art. If you stop indenting, your page will be less smaller in filesize and load faster. LOL I really think there are some people who believe search-engines would rank a site higher if it is perfectly indented. Minified and compressed HTML is much more valuable: less data, loads faster. So if your site has a lots of visitors, it has to be delivered Minified/compressed.</p>
<p>Also, you are using UTF-8, so you don&#8217;t need to use HTML-entities, isnt it?</p>
<p>I also think it is very evil to put the script-tags at the end of the body; it should be loaded inside the head-tags. I see you are using jQuery, so you just should use the ready event.</p>

          </div>

    <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-64468">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64468">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/24e08a9ea84deb17ae121074d0f17125?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">

        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Mathias Bynens</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64468"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T05:26:18-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p><code>&lt;aside&gt;&lt;h3&gt;&#x2026;&lt;/h3&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;</code> is far from beautiful if you read the HTML5 spec. Inside sectioning elements, reset your headers and start anew using h1.</p>
<p>By the way, this sites comment escaper fails:</p>
<blockquote><p>Wrap all (unescaped) code in &lt;code&gt; tags. (single or multiple lines).</p></blockquote>
<p>Why do you ask for unescaped code when you really mean <em>escaped</em> code?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-64473">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64473">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/d5c231834a8ba99843c4e2f870684bb8?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">WebCoder</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64473"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T06:24:24-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>

          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Umm.. Read this and had to comment&#8230; You appear to be saying how clean your code is, but there are still pretty bad habits in there.</p>
<p>* Your CSS hacks in the header for IE are NOT W3C standard &#8211; even though they would pass a W3C check they are still a hack.<br />
* Your paths are mostly ABSOLUTE, rather than relative. You are only using relative paths in your PHP includes.<br />
* Your use of utf encoding for apostrophes is not necessary.<br />
* They are PHP includes, NOT Server Side Includes &#8211; there is a big difference.<br />
* If you turn the php parser on for every html page your server will slow down, unless you&#8217;re saying that your page is PHP&#8230; In which case it isn&#8217;t beautiful HTML, it&#8217;s &#8216;beautiful&#8217; PHP.<br />
* Google analytics is an unnecessary php include &#8211; the tracking code is pretty standard and to anyone who has done a website or two it&#8217;s pretty clear what it is.<br />
* Your saying &#8216;col&#8217; is a good, clear, name for a css class?</p>

    <li class="comment odd alt depth-2" id="li-comment-64491">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64491">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/0473b141f15bc2725b1520814aa12c93?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Davin Studer</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64491"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:32:45-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p><em>&#8220;&#8230;though they would pass a W3C check they are still a hack.&#8221;</em><br />
Conditional comments are not a hack.  They are exactly that comments.  IE just interprets them as something else.  The start hack &#8230; now that is a a hack because it messes with a bug in the rendering engine.  Conditional comments are not a bug.  They were planned that way.  If you want to say that conditional comments are a hack, then you must say that the Webkit CSS animation/transition stuff is a hack, since it is not in the spec yet.  Taking advantage of a legitimate browser feature is never a hack.</p>
<p><em>&#8220;They are PHP includes, NOT Server Side Includes  there is a big difference.&#8221;</em><br />
There is not a BIG difference between php includes and server side includes.  They are basically the same things and honestly your are nitpicking.  It is a commonly accepted thing to call them server side includes because they are included on the server rather than the client.</p>
<p><em>&#8220;In which case it isnt beautiful HTML, its beautiful PHP.&#8221;</em><br />
No it is still HTML.  PHP is only the stuff within the PHP tags.  Everything else is still HTML.  The PHP parser doesn&#8217;t mess with or bother with anything outside of the PHP tags.</p>
<p><em>Google analytics is an unnecessary php include  the tracking code is pretty standard and to anyone who has done a website or two its pretty clear what it is.</em><br />
Not necessarily.  Depends on your reason for putting it into an include.  What if you want to have some code in the include that checks the page name and selectively puts in the tracking code.  So, you could have the code show on some pages but not others.  That is just one example, I could think of others.</p>
<p><em>&#8220;Your saying col is a good, clear, name for a css class?&#8221;</em><br />
Sure, if it is a column and you want all your columns to look the same.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

            <p>Actually &#8220;col&#8221; is still presentational.  The HTML is telling me this should be a column.  On a phone it probably shouldn&#8217;t be a column.</p>
<p>On the other hand I&#8217;m not sure we can 100% get away from it.  Something has to tell the browser that its going into tab, dialog or column.</p>
<p>I&#8217;m playing around with a site I&#8217;m trying to make 0% presentational and what I ended up doing was giving each block (section since I&#8217;m using HTML5) an ID, then using jQuery to .addClass(&#8220;.dialog&#8221;) to those sections that are intended to be dialogs.</p>
<p>In the iPhone javascript, I don&#8217;t insert the class since it has no meaning there (the styles for .dialog are for browsers only).</p>

    <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-64474">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64474">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/1c53a39881ddd4d95144eeb614172b79?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Sean</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64474"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T06:27:05-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Putting scripts at the end of the body should ensure that the html gets loaded before javascript. When javascript files are being downloaded and executed, they can often result in other downloads stalling. So when you put them in the head you are running the risk of displaying a blank page while your javascript loads.</p>
<p>Putting them at the end of the body eliminates this problem. This ensures that content isn&#8217;t waiting on the presentation layer.</p>
<p>That being said, I do put js in the head when the content relies on it, such as loading a SWF file or launching a mapping application.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64496">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64496">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/0473b141f15bc2725b1520814aa12c93?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Davin Studer</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64496"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T09:40:45-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I would assume the above example is not intended to be a hard and fast rule of where to place elements semantically on a page.  Rather it is <em>an</em> example of a clean and easy to read layout.  I doubt the author is saying he stumbled onto the best way to create a layout.  Rather I would think he is just trying to make the case that clean code makes a pretty site and an easy to read one as well.  Where you put a section element is really going to depend upon your site.  If you stick it in side an article elements you may shoot yourself in the foot CSS wise.  Each site needs to be evaluated for what you are trying to do with the site, but we do need to stay somewhat within the realm of accepted practice.  But there is some fudge room.  For instance I never put a wrapper class around everything within the body tag.  But I see sites do this all the time.  I&#8217;m not going to cry foul.  I think it is unnecessary, since you already have a wrapper &#8230; it&#8217;s called the body element.  But many people do it &#8230; fudge room.</p>

          </div>
        </div>

    <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-64520">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64520">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/f28fbef2f056e55227bc8c6a5a247dc6?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Paul</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64520"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T14:30:09-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>I think that was a great view of what HTML should look like About time someone made note that you should only have one stylesheet. I would go one further and say you don&#8217;t need conditional ones anymore. If you play around with a site for long enough, you can get around most things these days, even for IE6. You shouldn&#8217;t have to hack IE7,  in my experience.</p>
<p>Semantic Classes were also another great thing you pointed out, people don&#8217;t think about the next man trying to edit their code sometimes! </p>
<p>Which would be my only other gripe &#8211; multiple classes on one element. This can be a complete ballache if you&#8217;re trying to work on someone else&#8217;s code and you have to look in 10 different places to figure what all the classes are doing.</p>
<p>Great article, thanks for posting up.</p>

          </div>

    <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-64523">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64523">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/6549dd554fca958976338e90aad17510?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">John</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64523"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-10T16:37:52-07:00">November 10, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>It&#8217;s worth noting that, if code is indeed poetry, then this should be taken as one person&#8217;s specific vision, and not necessarily one that everyone should hold themselves to.</p>
<p>I&#8217;m not arguing that the suggestions are invalid, but that beauty is in the eye of the beholder!</p>
<p>***</p>
<p>I work with another language extensively, so I can attest to the fact that it&#8217;s very important to encode special characters.</p>
<p>It doesn&#8217;t matter how you declare your document or how you save it in your editor; someone will find a way to view the page in an unintended encoding. Encoding special characters bypasses the whole mess that can result (weird characters in the best case scenario, and broken pages in the worst).</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

            <div class="comment-author">Davin Studer</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64570"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-11T09:44:37-07:00">November 11, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>The US is not the only country that uses mm/dd/yyyy. So, by that definition it is international. I don&#8217;t think we need to get that picky in web design. If we start down that road then maybe we should standardize on US or British English as well. I think the rule is use the formatting and rules that apply to your constituents. If you have international constituents then you might need to provide a multi-lingual site that also uses the date format of that particular country.</p>

          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Very many (most European?) countries use dd/mm/yyyy or some variation with DD <em>before</em> MM, so Peter has a very good point, although Americans aren&#8217;t the only ones using the MM first.</p>
<p>Even though I am American (living in Europe) I must concur his point. UNLESS your site is for a specific region where you know your visitors will understand the date format, a more standard and easy to understand format should be used, i.e. 12 November 2009 or 12 Nov 2009.</p>
<p>Avoids any confusion, even if it does take up a bit more space.</p>

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/787ac4995dba4f50e8c536ee0cf74df1?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Basil</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-618749"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2013-10-29T22:35:58-07:00">October 29, 2013</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>@Davin Studer  Actually, the <em>only</em> other country to use mm/dd/yyyy besides the US is Belize. See <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Date_format_by_country" rel="nofollow">Date format by country</a>.</p>
<p>This simplest solution to the date format problem is to use the international standard <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISO_8601" rel="nofollow">ISO 8601</a>, recognizable everywhere without ambiguity: YYYY-MM-DD</p>
<p>Example: 2013-10-29</p>

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/6774588d07df3ca5c5663e82d61f0385?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">

            <div class="comment-author">Grant Z</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64700"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-13T11:28:04-07:00">November 13, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Love it! Why do you need to specify height and width on an image. That seems old school to me. Doesn&#8217;t anyone here know?</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>

  <ul class="children">

    <li class="comment even depth-2" id="li-comment-64703">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64703">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/697c7f4ee06b6c547dd951aef547e439?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">Russell Heimlich</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64703"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-13T11:41:36-07:00">November 13, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>It has to do with how the browser renders the page. Adding width and height lets the browser block out the right space for the image. Otherwise it figures out the dimensions from the image metadata as it is transferred from the server to the client. </p>
<p>Bottom line is it causes less judder as the page loads.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

    <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-64898">

      <div class="comment-wrap" id="comment-64898">

        <div class="author-avatar">
          <img src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAP///wAAACH5BAAAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAICRAEAOw==" width="50" height="50" class="lazyload-gravatar" alt="User Avatar" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/ce1e0923869ac905cdb952292040357e?s=160&d=retro&r=pg">
          <svg class="half-circle" width="80px" height="80px">
            <use xlink:href="#half-circle"></use>
          </svg>
        </div>

        <div class="comment-body">

          <div class="comment-author-wrap vcard">
            <div class="comment-author">James Ellis</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-64898"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2009-11-17T16:10:32-07:00">November 17, 2009</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>&#8220;File Paths  Site resources use relative file paths for efficiency&#8221;</p>
<p>That idea breaks down when you want to serve your assets from different domains or even subdomains. For instance if you have a high traffic site and want to spread the load.  Use absolute paths to point at the various content locations on your content delivery network. e.g static2.example.com/css/main.css</p>
<p>&#8220;Use one css file&#8221;<br />
This is a good idea speed wise as the browser only has to make one connection to download your file. For modularisation it&#8217;s a complete PITA. The best solution is to write modular CSS files and bung them together into one file at runtime using a server side handler. That way you can server up all your CSS parts as one file that can also be cached and compressed (and cache it so its only hit once).</p>
<p>Try reading the YSlow documentation for more info.</p>
<p>&#8220;Indenting&#8221; &#8211; for sure your code needs to be readable especially in view-source. Using gzip or deflate compression will reduce the need for taking whitespace out of your code.</p>

          </div>
        </div>
        <div class="comment-actions">
          <div class="reply">
                      </div>
        </div>

      </div>

            <div class="comment-author">Todd B</div> <div class="comment-time"><a href="https://css-tricks.com/what-beautiful-html-code-looks-like/#comment-67509"><span class="screen-reader">Permalink to comment</span>#</a> <time datetime="2010-01-05T10:56:31-07:00">January 5, 2010</time></div>
            
          </div>
          <div class="comment-content">

            <p>Great update Chris, thanks. I think this is relevant to what @paul was getting at:</p>
<p>I like to use page variables to declare many things, including the body id. So you can declare: </p>
<p><code>$activepage = 'about-us';</code></p>
<p>Then you can echo the value to the body id as well as use it in other places like inside of includes, etc.</p>
